**User**

So now im in the final steps of completing this graph rag model. This is the tgesting phase. I have additionally developed this with a different LLM , so you can look at the changes made. Here is the  testing protocol suggested for this project in order  to be research  grade : " Research Evaluation Protocol: V3 vs Traditional RAG
To definitively prove V3's superiority in your research paper, you must design tests that explicitly target the known architectural flaws of Traditional RAG (which relies purely on vector similarity).

This protocol outlines the experimental design, metrics, and adversarial test cases needed to generate compelling quantitative results.

1. The Core Experiment: The "Tokens-to-Truth" Iteration Test
This is based on your excellent suggestion. Complex legal queries often require exploring multiple concepts. Traditional RAG fails on the first try because it lacks the context. An iterative agent (like a human with a chatbot or an Agentic RAG loop) must ask follow-up questions to eventually gather the full picture.

The Hypothesis: V3 resolves multi-hop legal queries in a single LLM generation call with a highly optimized context window. Traditional RAG requires multiple iterative turns (subqueries generated by an LLM loop) to reach the same level of accuracy, aggressively burning tokens and time.

Methodology
Input: Give an identical, complex multi-hop prompt to both systems. (e.g., "Under what conditions is the Company exempt from indemnifying the Licensee for IP infringement, and what is the maximum financial cap?")
V3 Pipeline:
Runs its internal query decomposition, retrieves via FAISS, expands cross-references, optimizes via CSS.
Executes one LLM generation call.
Traditional RAG Pipeline (Iterative Agent):
Retrieves the top-k chunks based on the original query.
Executes an LLM generation call.
Evaluation: If the answer is incomplete, a separate "evaluator prompt" generates a new subquery based on what's missing (e.g., "Now search for: Indemnity Financial Cap").
Retrieves top-k for the subquery, concatenates with previous context, calls the LLM again.
Loops until the standard is met or a max-turn limit (e.g., 5 turns) is reached.
Quantitative Metrics to Report
Prompt Iterations to Ground Truth: (V3 should average 1.0; TRAG will average 3+)
Total Prompt Tokens Used: (V3's upfront graph optimization vs TRAG's compounding conversation history)
Total Completion Tokens Used: (V3 generates one answer; TRAG generates multiple intermediate partial answers).
End-to-End Latency: Time taken to produce the final correct answer.
2. Adversarial Test Scenarios (The "Proof of Concept")
You need a benchmark query set that specifically forces the models into situations where vector similarity is mathematically insufficient.

Create a dataset of 20-50 queries categorized into these three "trap" types:

Trap A: The "Invisible Exception"
Mechanism: A core operational clause (Section 4.1) looks like a perfect match for the query. However, a different section (Section 9.5) explicitly overrides it ("Notwithstanding Section 4.1...").
Why V3 Wins: The precomputed_cross_ref_edges link 4.1 directly to 9.5. The CSS optimizer pulls 9.5 into the graph automatically. Furthermore, the contradiction_flagger detects "Notwithstanding" and injects a warning into the prompt.
Why TRAG Fails: Section 9.5 has low vector similarity to the user's query about the operational details. TRAG never retrieves it.
Research Metric: Fatal Error Rate (percentage of answers that are legally incorrect because an overriding clause was missed).
Trap B: The "Distant Definition" (Bridge Node)
Mechanism: The user asks about a restrictive covenant that uses an obscure capitalized term defined 40 pages earlier in Article 1 (e.g., "Change of Control Event").
Why V3 Wins: The entity extraction recognizes the defined term. V3 retrieves the covenant chunk and the definition chunk. If the CSS optimizer tries to prune the definition chunk, the bridge-node protection prevents it, recognizing it as critical structural glue.
Why TRAG Fails: The definition of "Change of Control Event" consists of generic financial jargon that has no vector similarity to the specific restrictive covenant query. TRAG leaves the definition behind, forcing the LLM to hallucinate the meaning.
Research Metric: RAGAS Faithfulness and RAGAS Context Recall.
Trap C: The "Scattered Components" (Subquery Synthesis)
Mechanism: A query asking for a cumulative list: "List all termination triggers for cause." The triggers are spread across Sections 8.1(a), 8.1(b), and a separate Schedule annexed to the contract.
Why V3 Wins: The 
query_preprocessor
 explicitly breaks this into parallel subqueries. The CSS 
subquery_coverage
 feature forces the optimizer to keep chunks that answer different parts of the prompt, maximizing diversity.
Why TRAG Fails: High similarity chunks cluster heavily around the single most relevant paragraph (e.g., Section 8.1(a)), pushing out the Schedule entirely due to the strict top_k cutoff. It suffers from "Information Redundancy."
Research Metric: Recall Precision and Information Diversity Penalty.
3. Recommended Industry Standard Metrics (RAGAS)
To make your paper academically unassailable, use the established RAGAS (Retrieval Augmented Generation Assessment) framework on the final outputs of both systems:

Context Recall: To what extent does the retrieved context align with the annotated true answer? (V3 will dominate here due to structural expansion).
Context Precision: Is the context useful, or mostly noise? (V3 will score higher because the greedy optimizer prunes irrelevant nodes before prompting the LLM, whereas TRAG sends a fixed block of text).
Faithfulness: Is the generated answer derived entirely from the provided context? (TRAG will score lower because it will hallucinate definitions it failed to retrieve).
Answer Relevancy: How effectively does the answer address the prompt?
Conclusion for the Paper
The narrative of your results section should be: "Traditional RAG requires external, high-latency iterative agent loops to brute-force its way through complex document topologies. V3 internalizes this topology into an offline graph, allowing it to navigate the document structure via edge logic rather than raw semantic guessing, achieving ground-truth accuracy in a single, token-efficient pass." ". So based off all this , evaluate critically and tell me if this is the correct way to go aboyt said testing and make a plan. Else tell me where are the issues

## Critical Evaluation of the Testing Protocol

I've thoroughly reviewed every module in your V3 codebase. The testing protocol is **conceptually strong** — the three "Trap" categories and the Tokens-to-Truth framing are excellent for a research narrative. However, there are **serious gaps between what the protocol promises and what the code currently delivers**. If you go to evaluation now, you will produce results that don't support the claims, and a reviewer will dismantle the paper.

Here's my honest breakdown:

---

### 🔴 CRITICAL ISSUES (Will Invalidate Results)

#### 1. **The Iterative TRAG Agent Doesn't Exist**

The protocol describes a sophisticated iterative agent for Traditional RAG that generates subqueries, evaluates partial answers, and loops until convergence. But your `TraditionalRAG` class is a single-shot pipeline:

```76:106:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\evaluation\rag_baselines\traditional_rag.py
    def answer(self, query: str, k: int = 3) -> dict:
        """Full RAG pipeline: retrieve + generate answer."""
        start_time = time.time()
        
        # Retrieve relevant chunks
        retrieved = self.retrieve(query, k=k)
        # ... single retrieve, single generate ...
```

**You cannot measure "Prompt Iterations to Ground Truth" if the TRAG system has no iteration loop.** You're currently comparing V3 (multi-step optimizer) against TRAG (single-shot), which actually makes TRAG look *more efficient*, not less.

**Fix:** You need to implement an `IterativeTraditionalRAG` class with an evaluator loop that detects incomplete answers, generates follow-up subqueries, re-retrieves, and accumulates context. This is the central experiment of your paper.

#### 2. **No Ground Truth Answers**

The protocol repeatedly says "ground truth" but you have **no gold-standard reference answers** anywhere. Your `cuad_eval_dataset.py` only has keyword lists:

```19:136:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\data\cuad_eval_dataset.py
CUAD_EVAL_QUERIES: List[Dict] = [
    {
        "query": "What are the termination conditions in this contract?",
        "category": "termination",
        "expected_entities": ["termination", "terminate", "breach", "expiration"],
        "answer_keywords": ["terminate", "breach", "notice", "days"],
        "difficulty": "single_hop",
    },
    # ...
```

**Without expert-annotated reference answers (specific to each contract), you cannot compute RAGAS Context Recall (which requires ground truth), you cannot measure "Fatal Error Rate" (which requires knowing the correct answer), and you have no way to determine "iterations to ground truth."**

**Fix:** For at least 20–30 queries across 5–10 CUAD contracts, manually extract the correct answer text from the contract, including the specific section references. This is non-negotiable for research-grade evaluation.

#### 3. **The `contradiction_flagger` Does Not Exist**

The protocol for Trap A claims: *"the contradiction_flagger detects 'Notwithstanding' and injects a warning into the prompt."* But your contradiction detection is a stub that always returns `0.0`:

```8:12:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\tools\contradiction_stub.py
class ContradictionStub(BaseContradiction):
    """Always returns 0.0 for contradiction."""

    def score(self, text_a: str, text_b: str) -> float:
        return 0.0
```

And CSS_final explicitly zeroes it out:

```384:389:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\css\calculator.py
    values["css_final"] = values["css_score"]
    
    # Keep stubs zeroed for backward compatibility of return dict
    values["semantic"] = 1.0
    values["contradict"] = 0.0
```

**You cannot claim V3 wins on Trap A via contradiction detection if that feature doesn't work.** However, V3 *can* still win on Trap A via cross-reference edges alone — you just need to reframe the claim.

#### 4. **RAGAS Metrics Are Not Correctly Implemented**

Your `ragas_context_recall` uses the generated answer as a proxy for ground truth. The docstring even acknowledges this:

```377:378:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\evaluation\metrics_calculator.py
    Note: Official RAGAS uses ground truth, but we use the answer as proxy.
```

This is **circular reasoning** — you're checking if the answer is supported by the context that generated the answer. It will always score high. Similarly, you're missing `ragas_context_precision` entirely. A reviewer familiar with RAGAS will reject this.

**Fix:** Either use the real RAGAS library (`pip install ragas`) with proper ground truth, or implement the metrics correctly with your manual annotations.

---

### 🟡 SIGNIFICANT ISSUES (Will Weaken the Paper)

#### 5. **Bridge-Node Protection Exists But Has a Major Limitation**

Good news: bridge-node protection IS implemented (Fix 2.12 in `prune.py`):

```66:73:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\transforms\prune.py
        # Fix 2.12: Bridge-node protection
        degree = get_node_degree(graph, node.id)
        is_bridge = degree >= 3 and has_cross_ref_edges(graph, node.id)
        
        # Protected nodes get a large relevance boost so they're not removed
        effective_score = relevance
        if is_bridge:
            effective_score += 0.5  # Strong protection
```

**However**, the condition `degree >= 3` is very strict. In a typical graph with 10 nodes, most definition nodes will have degree 1 or 2 (linked to the clause that uses them). They'll never reach degree 3. So your Trap B scenario — where a "Distant Definition" chunk is retrieved but has low query relevance — will likely see the definition **pruned anyway** because it fails the bridge-node check.

**Fix:** Lower the threshold or add a separate protection for nodes with `defined_term` edge types regardless of degree.

#### 6. **Query Preprocessor Falls Back to Rules in the Optimizer**

Your `preprocess_query` function in `tools/query_preprocessor.py` can use an LLM for smart decomposition, but in the actual optimizer pipeline (`_build_initial_retrieval_graph`), no `llm_client` is passed:

```62:71:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\policy\optimizer.py
    try:
        from tools.query_preprocessor import preprocess_query
        preprocessor_output = preprocess_query(query)
        entities = preprocessor_output.get("entities", [])
        domain_terms = preprocessor_output.get("domain_terms", [])
```

Since no `llm_client` is provided, it falls back to the `ParserStub` which generates naive subqueries like `"What is termination?"`:

```167:169:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\tools\parser_stub.py
        for entity in entities[:5]:  # Limit to avoid explosion
            subqueries.append(f"What is {entity}?")
```

This means Trap C's claim — *"the query_preprocessor explicitly breaks this into parallel subqueries"* — is only working at a very basic level. The subquery decomposition V3 does is barely better than keyword extraction.

**Fix:** Wire the LLM client into the preprocessor call during optimize, or accept that subquery decomposition is rule-based and adjust the claims accordingly.

#### 7. **Statistical Design Is Missing**

The protocol says 20–50 queries but doesn't mention:
- **Statistical significance tests** (paired t-test, Wilcoxon signed-rank)
- **Confidence intervals** for all metrics
- **Effect size** measures (Cohen's d)
- **Multiple comparison correction** (Bonferroni or Holm) since you're comparing across many metrics
- **Inter-annotator agreement** for ground truth labels (Cohen's kappa)
- **Variance** across different contracts (you need to test on multiple CUAD contracts, not just one)

A research paper needs all of these.

#### 8. **The Fixed Corpus Is Not Legal**

Your `fixed_corpus.json` contains generic renewable energy documents ("Solar Energy Basics"), not legal contracts. The entire testing protocol is designed for legal contracts (CUAD). The existing test scripts (`test_graph_rag.py`, `test_traditional_rag.py`) use the default corpus, not CUAD.

---

### 🟢 WHAT'S ACTUALLY GOOD

1. **The three "Trap" categories are architecturally sound** — they do target real weaknesses of vector-only retrieval
2. **Cross-reference edge pre-computation** (`precompute_cross_ref_edges`) is genuinely V3's strongest feature and will help with Trap A
3. **Cost tracking infrastructure** (`CostTracker`, `ComparisonCostTracker`) is solid and ready
4. **The `terminal_comparison.py` harness** is a good starting point for the comparison
5. **CSS subquery_coverage** is a genuine advantage for Trap C (information diversity)
6. **FAISS + pre-computed embeddings + IDF** is a genuinely better retrieval architecture than TRAG's Chroma

---

### 📋 RECOMMENDED PLAN (Priority Order)

Here's what I'd do, in order:

| # | Task | Why | Effort |
|---|------|-----|--------|
| **1** | **Create Gold-Standard Annotations** | Without ground truth, nothing else matters. Pick 5 CUAD contracts, write 6–8 queries per contract (covering all 3 trap types), manually extract correct answers with section citations. | 2–3 days |

| **3** | **Implement Proper RAGAS** | Either use the `ragas` library directly or fix your implementations to use ground truth. Add `context_precision`. | 0.5 day |

| **5** | **Relax Bridge-Node Protection** | Lower degree threshold from 3→1 for nodes with `defined_term` edges. This strengthens Trap B claims. | 1 hour |
| **6** | **Add "Notwithstanding" Detection** | Simple regex in `edge_builder.py` to detect overriding clauses. Doesn't need full NLI — just flag "Notwithstanding Section X" patterns as cross-ref edges with a `contradiction` type. | 2 hours |
| **7** | **Build Automated Benchmark Runner** | A script that runs all 30+ queries through both systems automatically, collects all metrics, computes statistical significance (scipy.stats), and outputs a LaTeX-ready table. | 1 day |
| **8** | **Reframe Claims to Match Reality** | Adjust the protocol's language: V3 wins via *structural graph traversal* (cross-refs, IDF entities), NOT via contradiction detection or real NLI. Be honest about stubs. | 1 hour |

**Total estimated effort: ~5–6 days.**

The paper's core narrative — *"V3 internalizes document topology into an offline graph, navigating via edge logic rather than semantic guessing"* — is **genuinely supported** by your cross-reference edges, IDF entity overlap, and CSS subquery coverage. You just need to close the gap between the protocol's aspirational claims and the code's actual capabilities, and build the missing evaluation infrastructure.

## 1. The "Iterative RAG" / Checklist Approach

**Your idea has merit but the "V3 as golden standard" variant is fatally flawed. The checklist variant is viable with modifications.**

Here's why:

**V3 as golden standard = circular reasoning.** If you evaluate TRAG by comparing it against V3's output, your paper is literally saying "V3 is better because V3 is the benchmark." Any reviewer will immediately reject this. You cannot be both the contestant and the judge.

**The "checklist" variant is actually strong**, but the checklist must come from the **document itself**, not from V3's output. Here's the correct way to think about it:

For a complex query like *"Under what conditions is the Company exempt from indemnifying the Licensee for IP infringement, and what is the maximum financial cap?"*, you go to the actual CUAD contract and manually extract:

- ✅ Section 8.2 defines indemnification obligations
- ✅ Section 8.2(c) lists the IP infringement exemption  
- ✅ Section 8.5 defines the financial cap
- ✅ Section 1.15 defines "IP Infringement" (used in 8.2 but defined elsewhere)
- ✅ Section 12.3 overrides the cap in cases of willful misconduct ("Notwithstanding Section 8.5...")

That's your checklist — **5 information units derived from the contract, not from either system's output**. Then you measure:

| Metric | V3 | TRAG Query 1 | TRAG Query 2 | TRAG Query 3 |
|--------|----|----|----|----|
| Info units retrieved | 5/5 | 2/5 | 3/5 | 4/5 |

This is methodologically sound because the checklist is **independently verifiable** by a third party reading the contract.

**However**, this means you need to do the manual annotation work. There's no shortcut. You need a human (you) reading contracts and extracting these checklists. This is Issue #2 (ground truth), which you've agreed needs to happen.

One practical approach to reduce the burden: **for the "how many additional queries does TRAG need" experiment**, you can automate the TRAG side. After TRAG's first query, use an LLM evaluator to check which checklist items are missing, then auto-generate a follow-up query targeting the missing items. This is your iterative agent, but it's simpler than what was originally described — it's just a loop that says "you missed the financial cap, now search for that."

**Verdict: The approach is valid IF the checklist comes from manual contract annotation, not from V3's output.**

---

## 2. Ground Truth

You agree, so I'll just note: **this is the single highest-priority blocker.** Everything else (statistical testing, RAGAS, the checklist experiment) depends on having ground truth annotations. Start here.

For practical scope: annotate **5 contracts × 6 queries each = 30 query-answer pairs**. Each query-answer pair should include:
- The query
- The correct answer (textual)
- The specific section references that contain the answer
- Which "trap type" it belongs to (A/B/C)
- The information unit checklist (for the Tokens-to-Truth experiment)

30 pairs is the bare minimum for statistical significance with large effect sizes.

---

## 3 & 4. Contradiction Flagger and RAGAS — "Implementation Issues"

**Partially correct, but let me be precise about the implications.**

**#3 (Contradiction Flagger):** Yes, implementing it is straightforward — you'd add "Notwithstanding" regex detection to `edge_builder.py`. But here's what you need to decide: **do you claim Trap A works via contradiction detection, or via cross-reference edges?**

Looking at your `edge_builder.py`, the cross-reference patterns already catch "Notwithstanding Section X" because of this pattern:

```25:28:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\graph\edge_builder.py
CROSS_REF_PATTERNS = [
    re.compile(r'(?:subject to|pursuant to|as (?:defined|described|set forth) in|see|under|per)\s+'
               r'(?:Section|Article|Exhibit|Schedule|Appendix)\s+[\dIVXLCA-Z]+(?:\.\d+)*',
               re.IGNORECASE),
```

The problem is "Notwithstanding" isn't in this list (`subject to|pursuant to|as defined in|...`). So if a clause says "Notwithstanding Section 4.1, the cap shall not apply in cases of fraud," the edge builder **won't detect it** because "Notwithstanding" isn't a listed trigger word.

This is a 1-line fix (add `notwithstanding` to the regex), but it changes the Trap A results from "V3 also misses it" to "V3 catches it." So yes it's an implementation issue, but it's a **results-critical** implementation issue, not just cleanup.

**#4 (RAGAS):** This is more than implementation — it's a **methodology** choice. You have two options:

- **Option A:** Use the real `ragas` Python library (`pip install ragas`). It handles context recall, context precision, faithfulness, and answer relevancy correctly. But it requires ground truth and makes OpenAI API calls (cost).
- **Option B:** Keep your custom implementations but fix them. Specifically, `ragas_context_recall` MUST use ground truth (your annotations from #2), not the generated answer.

I'd recommend Option A if budget allows — it's the industry standard and reviewers will trust `ragas` library scores more than custom implementations.

**Verdict: Yes these are implementation issues, but #3 is results-critical (add 1 regex pattern), and #4 requires the ground truth from #2 before it can be fixed.**

---

## 5. Bridge-Node Protection Threshold

**Your question: how to determine the right value?**

This is an empirical question. Here's the principled approach:

**Don't guess. Measure it on your annotated data.** Specifically:

1. Run V3 on your 30 annotated queries
2. After each run, check: did the optimizer prune a node that contained ground-truth information?
3. For each pruned ground-truth node, check its degree and edge type

This tells you the actual distribution. If you find that definition nodes (Trap B) typically have degree 1–2 with `defined_term` edges, then you know degree ≥ 3 is too strict.

**The more principled fix** is to not use degree at all for defined-term edges. Instead, protect any node that has a `defined_term` or `cross_ref` edge type, regardless of degree. The logic is: *"if the document author explicitly defined this term, it's structurally important."*

So rather than tuning a threshold, change the condition from:

```python
is_bridge = degree >= 3 and has_cross_ref_edges(graph, node.id)
```

to something like:

```python
has_structural_edges = has_cross_ref_edges(graph, node.id)
is_high_degree_bridge = degree >= 3 and has_structural_edges
is_definition_node = any("defined_term" in e.relation for e in graph.edges 
                         if e.source == node.id or e.target == node.id)
is_bridge = is_high_degree_bridge or is_definition_node
```

This way, definition nodes are always protected, and high-degree cross-ref nodes are also protected. You don't need to tune a numeric threshold — the protection is **type-based** rather than **count-based**.

**Verdict: Don't tune a number. Change the logic to protect by edge type (defined_term, cross_ref) rather than by degree count. Then validate empirically that your Trap B test cases retain their definition chunks.**

---

## 7. Statistical Testing — Your Main Concern

**This is both a dataset thing AND a general methodology thing. Let me break it down concretely.**

### What You Need (General Methodology)

For any research paper comparing System A vs System B:

| Requirement | What It Means | How You Do It |
|---|---|---|
| **Sample size** | Enough data points per metric to detect a difference | ≥30 queries (you have 15 in `cuad_eval_dataset.py`, need to double it) |
| **Paired comparison** | Same query goes to both systems | Already doing this in `terminal_comparison.py` ✅ |
| **Significance test** | "Is the difference real or just noise?" | Paired t-test or Wilcoxon signed-rank test |
| **Effect size** | "How big is the difference?" | Cohen's d (small=0.2, medium=0.5, large=0.8) |
| **Confidence intervals** | "What's the range of plausible values?" | 95% CI on mean difference |
| **Multiple comparisons** | "Am I cherry-picking the metric where I win?" | Bonferroni correction: if testing 6 metrics, significance threshold becomes 0.05/6 = 0.0083 |

### How to Implement This Concretely

You'd add a function like this to your evaluation pipeline:

```python
from scipy import stats
import numpy as np

def compute_significance(v3_scores: list, trag_scores: list, metric_name: str):
    """Compute statistical significance for a single metric."""
    v3 = np.array(v3_scores)
    trag = np.array(trag_scores)
    
    # Paired test (same queries)
    # Use Wilcoxon for small samples or non-normal distributions
    if len(v3) >= 30:
        t_stat, p_value = stats.ttest_rel(v3, trag)  # paired t-test
    else:
        t_stat, p_value = stats.wilcoxon(v3, trag)  # non-parametric
    
    # Effect size (Cohen's d for paired samples)
    diff = v3 - trag
    cohens_d = np.mean(diff) / np.std(diff, ddof=1)
    
    # 95% CI on mean difference
    mean_diff = np.mean(diff)
    se = stats.sem(diff)
    ci_low, ci_high = stats.t.interval(0.95, len(diff)-1, loc=mean_diff, scale=se)
    
    return {
        "metric": metric_name,
        "v3_mean": float(np.mean(v3)),
        "trag_mean": float(np.mean(trag)),
        "mean_diff": float(mean_diff),
        "p_value": float(p_value),
        "cohens_d": float(cohens_d),
        "ci_95": (float(ci_low), float(ci_high)),
        "significant_at_005": p_value < 0.05,
    }
```

### Is It a Dataset Thing?

**Partially.** The CUAD dataset gives you contracts, but it doesn't give you ready-made RAG evaluation queries with multi-hop ground truth. CUAD's original annotations are for clause extraction (binary: does clause X exist?), not for the kind of multi-hop reasoning you're testing.

So you need to **create your own benchmark on top of CUAD**. This is normal — most RAG papers create their own evaluation sets. What makes it legitimate is:

1. You describe the annotation methodology in the paper
2. You report inter-annotator agreement (if possible, have a second person independently annotate 10 queries and compute Cohen's kappa)
3. You release the benchmark with the paper

If getting a second annotator isn't possible (common for capstone projects), you can mitigate by:
- Making the checklists very objective (specific section numbers, specific dollar amounts)
- Using factual queries where the answer is verifiable from the document
- Avoiding subjective queries like "Is this a fair contract?"

### Practical Steps

1. **Expand `cuad_eval_dataset.py`** to 30+ queries, with 10 per trap type
2. **Add ground truth fields**: `reference_answer`, `required_sections`, `information_units` (the checklist)
3. **Run both systems** on all queries, collect per-query scores
4. **Compute statistics** using the function above for each metric
5. **Report in the paper**: "V3 achieved significantly higher Context Recall (M=0.82, SD=0.11) than TRAG (M=0.54, SD=0.18), t(29)=7.3, p<0.001, d=1.85, 95% CI [0.20, 0.36]"

---

Yes, implementation issue. When running the actual evaluation, you load CUAD via the `cuad` shortcut in `terminal_comparison.py`. The fixed corpus is just for dev testing. This is fine as long as your final results are all on CUAD. Just make sure your paper **only reports CUAD numbers**, not fixed corpus numbers.

```python
from scipy import stats
import numpy as np

def compute_significance(v3_scores: list, trag_scores: list, metric_name: str):
    """Compute statistical significance for a single metric."""
    v3 = np.array(v3_scores)
    trag = np.array(trag_scores)
    
    # Paired test (same queries)
    # Use Wilcoxon for small samples or non-normal distributions
    if len(v3) >= 30:
        t_stat, p_value = stats.ttest_rel(v3, trag)  # paired t-test
    else:
        t_stat, p_value = stats.wilcoxon(v3, trag)  # non-parametric
    
    # Effect size (Cohen's d for paired samples)
    diff = v3 - trag
    cohens_d = np.mean(diff) / np.std(diff, ddof=1)
    
    # 95% CI on mean difference
    mean_diff = np.mean(diff)
    se = stats.sem(diff)
    ci_low, ci_high = stats.t.interval(0.95, len(diff)-1, loc=mean_diff, scale=se)
    
    return {
        "metric": metric_name,
        "v3_mean": float(np.mean(v3)),
        "trag_mean": float(np.mean(trag)),
        "mean_diff": float(mean_diff),
        "p_value": float(p_value),
        "cohens_d": float(cohens_d),
        "ci_95": (float(ci_low), float(ci_high)),
        "significant_at_005": p_value < 0.05,
    }
```

## "Notwithstanding" — What, Why, and How It Relates to V3

### What It Is (Legal Context)

In legal contracts, **"Notwithstanding"** is a term that means **"despite what is said elsewhere" or "regardless of anything to the contrary."** It's the legal equivalent of an override or exception.

Here's a concrete example from a typical contract:

> **Section 8.2 (Indemnification):** The Company shall indemnify the Licensee against all claims arising from intellectual property infringement, up to a maximum liability of $500,000.

> **Section 12.3 (Exceptions):** *Notwithstanding Section 8.2*, the liability cap shall not apply in cases of willful misconduct or fraud by the Company, and the Company's liability in such cases shall be unlimited.

Section 8.2 says the cap is $500K. Section 12.3 **overrides** that in specific circumstances. If someone asks *"What is the liability cap for IP infringement?"*, the correct answer is: *"$500K under Section 8.2, EXCEPT in cases of fraud where it's unlimited per Section 12.3."*

### Why It Matters for RAG Systems (Trap A)

This is exactly the **"Invisible Exception"** scenario (Trap A in your protocol). Here's what happens:

**Traditional RAG:**
1. User asks: *"What is the maximum liability for IP infringement?"*
2. TRAG embeds the query, searches the vector store
3. Section 8.2 has high semantic similarity to "liability" + "IP infringement" → it gets retrieved ✅
4. Section 12.3 talks about "willful misconduct" and "fraud" — words that have **low semantic similarity** to the query about "liability cap" → it does NOT get retrieved ❌
5. The LLM generates: *"The cap is $500,000"* — which is **legally incomplete and potentially wrong**

**V3 (intended behavior):**
1. Same query
2. V3 retrieves Section 8.2 via FAISS (same as TRAG)
3. **But** during graph construction, the edge builder detects that Section 8.2 is referenced by Section 12.3 (via the "Notwithstanding Section 8.2" pattern)
4. This creates a cross-reference edge between the two chunks
5. V3's expand step follows this edge and pulls Section 12.3 into the context
6. The LLM generates the complete answer including the exception

**This is the entire thesis of V3** — structural document relationships (cross-references, defined terms) matter more than semantic similarity for complex legal queries.

### What Was Intended in the Code

Looking at your codebase, there were **two layers** designed to handle this:

**Layer 1: Cross-Reference Edge Detection** (in `edge_builder.py`) — This is supposed to detect when one section references another and create an edge between them. Looking at the patterns:

```25:35:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\graph\edge_builder.py
CROSS_REF_PATTERNS = [
    re.compile(r'(?:subject to|pursuant to|as (?:defined|described|set forth) in|see|under|per)\s+'
               r'(?:Section|Article|Exhibit|Schedule|Appendix)\s+[\dIVXLCA-Z]+(?:\.\d+)*',
               re.IGNORECASE),
    re.compile(r'Section\s+\d+(?:\.\d+)*', re.IGNORECASE),
    re.compile(r'Article\s+[IVXLC]+', re.IGNORECASE),
    re.compile(r'Exhibit\s+[A-Z]', re.IGNORECASE),
    re.compile(r'Schedule\s+\d+', re.IGNORECASE),
    re.compile(r'Appendix\s+[A-Z]', re.IGNORECASE),
    re.compile(r'\bherein\b|\bhereinafter\b|\bhereunder\b', re.IGNORECASE),
]
```

The first pattern catches phrases like:
- "subject to Section 8.2" ✅
- "pursuant to Article V" ✅
- "as defined in Section 1.15" ✅
- "see Exhibit A" ✅

But it does **NOT** catch:
- "**Notwithstanding** Section 8.2" ❌
- "**Except as provided in** Section 8.2" ❌
- "**In addition to** Section 8.2" ❌

These override/exception phrases are arguably the **most important** cross-references in a legal document because they change the meaning of another clause. But they're missing from the regex.

**Layer 2: Contradiction Detection** (in `contradiction_stub.py`) — This was supposed to be a second safety net. If two chunks contradict each other (one says "$500K cap", another says "unlimited liability"), the system should flag the contradiction and ensure both are included. But it's a stub:

```8:12:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\tools\contradiction_stub.py
class ContradictionStub(BaseContradiction):
    """Always returns 0.0 for contradiction."""

    def score(self, text_a: str, text_b: str) -> float:
        return 0.0
```

And even if it weren't a stub, it's been disabled in CSS calculation:

```384:389:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\css\calculator.py
    values["css_final"] = values["css_score"]
    
    # Keep stubs zeroed for backward compatibility of return dict
    values["semantic"] = 1.0
    values["contradict"] = 0.0
```

### What Is Actually Happening Right Now

1. **Cross-reference edges ARE being pre-computed** during `FrozenState.build()`:

```123:127:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\core\frozen_state.py
        print(f"[FrozenState] Pre-computing cross-reference edges...")
        from graph.edge_builder import precompute_cross_ref_edges
        precomputed_edges = precompute_cross_ref_edges(corpus.documents)
        print(f"[FrozenState] Found {len(precomputed_edges)} cross-reference edges.")
```

2. **The expand transform DOES follow these edges**:

```31:64:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\transforms\expand.py
    for node in graph.nodes:
        if len(new_nodes) >= top_k:
            break
        for edge in precomputed:
            # ... if this node is connected via cross-ref, retrieve the target
            if target_id and target_id in doc_map:
                new_node = Node(
                    id=target_id,
                    text=doc.text,
                    metadata={"source": "cross_ref_expand", "title": doc.title},
                    confidence=0.85,
                )
                new_nodes.append(new_node)
```

3. **But** the detection is incomplete because "Notwithstanding" isn't in the regex pattern list. So if a contract's exception clause uses "Notwithstanding Section X" (which is extremely common — it's the standard legal phrasing for overrides), V3 **misses the edge entirely**.

4. **The second pattern** (`Section\s+\d+(?:\.\d+)*`) on line 29 catches bare section references like "Section 8.2" anywhere in text. So if Section 12.3 says "Notwithstanding Section 8.2...", the bare "Section 8.2" would be caught by the second pattern. However, `precompute_cross_ref_edges` then needs to **match** this reference to the actual chunk that represents Section 8.2, and this matching depends on `doc_section_ids`:

```512:514:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\graph\edge_builder.py
        if '_chunk_' in doc.id:
            match = re.match(r'(?:Section\s+)?(\d+(?:\.\d+)*)', doc.text.strip())
            doc_section_ids[doc.id] = match.group(1) if match else ''
```

This tries to extract a section ID from the **very start** of a chunk's text. If the chunk starts with "8.2 Indemnification..." it'll get "8.2". But many CUAD contract chunks won't start cleanly with a section number (they're split by character count/paragraphs in your chunker), so this matching is fragile.

**So the summary of what's actually happening:**
- Cross-ref detection exists but misses "Notwithstanding" patterns
- Even for patterns it does detect, matching references to actual chunks is fragile
- Contradiction detection is completely disabled (stub returns 0.0)
- The expand step WOULD follow cross-ref edges if they existed — the traversal logic is fine

**What needs to be fixed** (small changes, big impact):
- Add `notwithstanding|except as provided in|in addition to|without limiting` to the cross-ref regex
- Improve the section ID extraction in `precompute_cross_ref_edges` to be more robust to chunk boundaries

---

## Standard RAGAS Implementation

### What RAGAS Actually Is

RAGAS (Retrieval Augmented Generation Assessment) is a framework from [this paper](https://arxiv.org/abs/2309.15217) that defines four core metrics for evaluating RAG systems. It's become the de facto standard in the field.

### The Four RAGAS Metrics

| Metric | What It Measures | Inputs Required | How It's Computed |
|--------|-----------------|-----------------|-------------------|
| **Faithfulness** | Is the answer grounded in the context? (No hallucination) | answer + context | LLM extracts claims from answer → LLM checks each claim against context → proportion of supported claims |
| **Answer Relevancy** | Does the answer address the question? | question + answer | LLM generates N reverse-questions from the answer → embedding similarity between original question and generated questions → mean similarity |
| **Context Precision** | Is the retrieved context useful (not noise)? | question + context + **ground truth** | LLM checks if each context chunk is useful for deriving the ground truth answer → weighted precision (earlier useful chunks score higher) |
| **Context Recall** | Did retrieval find all the information needed? | context + **ground truth** | LLM breaks ground truth into claims → checks if each claim is supported by the retrieved context → proportion of ground truth claims found in context |

### What Your Code Currently Does vs What RAGAS Actually Does

**Faithfulness** — Your current implementation:

```69:103:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\evaluation\metrics_calculator.py
def faithfulness_score(answer: str, context: str) -> float:
    # ...
    # Split answer into sentences for fine-grained checking
    sentences = re.split(r'[.!?]+', answer)
    # ...
    context_vec = embedder.embed(context)
    # Check each sentence's similarity to context
    for sentence in sentences:
        sent_vec = embedder.embed(sentence)
        sim = _cosine_similarity(sent_vec, context_vec)
        total_score += max(0.0, sim)
    return min(1.0, total_score / len(sentences))
```

This uses **embedding cosine similarity** as a proxy. The problem: embeddings measure topical similarity, not logical entailment. A hallucinated sentence about "indemnification" will have high cosine similarity to context about "indemnification" even if the specific claims are fabricated.

**Real RAGAS Faithfulness** uses an LLM:
1. Extract specific claims from the answer (e.g., "The cap is $500,000", "The cap doesn't apply to fraud")
2. For each claim, ask the LLM: "Is this claim supported by the context? Yes/No"
3. Score = (supported claims) / (total claims)

**Context Recall** — Your current implementation:

```367:448:C:\Users\suyas\OneDrive\Desktop\v3_capstone\evaluation\metrics_calculator.py
def ragas_context_recall(answer: str, context: str) -> float:
    # Note: Official RAGAS uses ground truth, but we use the answer as proxy.
    # ...
    # Step 1: Extract claims from answer
    # Step 2: Check each claim against context
```

This checks if the *answer's* claims appear in context. But the answer was *generated from* the context, so this is circular — it will always score high.

**Real RAGAS Context Recall** needs ground truth:
1. Extract claims from the **ground truth answer** (not the generated answer)
2. Check if each ground truth claim is supported by the retrieved context
3. Score = (ground truth claims found in context) / (total ground truth claims)

This tells you: "Did the retrieval step actually find the information needed to produce the correct answer?"

**Context Precision** — You don't have this at all.

**Real RAGAS Context Precision:**
1. For each retrieved chunk, ask the LLM: "Is this chunk useful for answering the question, given the ground truth?"
2. Compute precision@k with useful chunks weighted higher when they appear earlier in the ranked list

### How to Use the Standard Library

The `ragas` library handles all of this. Here's what the integration looks like conceptually:

```python
from ragas import evaluate
from ragas.metrics import faithfulness, answer_relevancy, context_precision, context_recall
from datasets import Dataset

# Your evaluation data (from your annotations)
eval_data = {
    "question": ["What is the liability cap for IP infringement?", ...],
    "answer": ["The cap is $500K per Section 8.2...", ...],          # Generated by V3 or TRAG
    "contexts": [["Section 8.2: ...", "Section 12.3: ..."], ...],    # Retrieved chunks
    "ground_truth": ["The cap is $500K per Section 8.2, except...", ...],  # Your manual annotations
}

dataset = Dataset.from_dict(eval_data)

result = evaluate(
    dataset,
    metrics=[faithfulness, answer_relevancy, context_precision, context_recall],
)

# result gives you per-query scores for each metric
```

The library uses an LLM (OpenAI by default) to do the claim extraction and verification. It costs money per evaluation run (roughly $0.50–$2.00 for 30 queries depending on answer length), but it's the standard.

### What This Means for Your Project

You'd:
1. Replace your custom `faithfulness_score`, `ragas_context_recall`, and `ragas_answer_relevancy` with calls to the `ragas` library
2. Add `context_precision` (which you're currently missing)
3. Feed in your manual ground truth annotations
4. The library handles the LLM-based evaluation internally
5. Report the scores in your paper — reviewers will immediately recognize the RAGAS framework

The `ragas` library also supports using different LLMs as evaluators (not just OpenAI), so you can use it with your existing setup.

### Cost Estimate

For 30 queries × 2 systems (V3 + TRAG) × 4 metrics:
- ~240 LLM evaluation calls
- At GPT-4o-mini pricing: roughly $0.50–$1.00 total
- At GPT-4o pricing: roughly $3–$5 total

Very affordable for research-grade results.

RAGAS is the standard evaluation framework, and using the actual library (with your ground truth annotations) will make your paper far more credible than custom metric implementations. **Cursor**

Your instinct to go step-by-step is right. But the **ordering** has a subtle problem that I want to flag before you commit to it.

## The Problem with "Fixes First, Tests Second"

If you fix everything first and design tests second, you risk two things:

1. **You might fix things you don't need.** For example, if you decide during test design that you're NOT going to claim LLM-based query decomposition (and instead claim rule-based decomposition as a feature — "no extra LLM cost"), then wiring the LLM into the preprocessor is wasted work.

2. **You might miss fixes you DO need.** The test design will reveal requirements that aren't obvious from just reading the code. For example, when you sit down to annotate ground truth for a specific CUAD contract, you might discover that the chunker splits a critical section across two chunks in a way that breaks cross-reference matching — a fix you wouldn't have known about until you looked at real data.

## The Better Ordering

The actually correct sequence is:

```
1. Design tests & hypotheses (what are you claiming?)
         ↓
2. Create ground truth annotations (what does "correct" look like?)
         ↓
3. Fix the code (make the system work as claimed)
         ↓
4. Run tests & collect results
         ↓
5. Statistical analysis & write-up
```

**Why tests first?** Because the tests define your claims. Your claims define what the system needs to do. What the system needs to do defines which fixes matter.

For example:
- If your test design says "Trap A: V3 detects overriding clauses via cross-reference edges" → then the Notwithstanding regex fix is required
- If your test design says "We measure context recall using RAGAS" → then ground truth annotations are required, and the `ragas` library integration is required
- If your test design does NOT include a claim about contradiction detection → then the `ContradictionStub` doesn't need to be fixed at all, saving you time

## What I'd Actually Recommend

Combine steps 1 and 3 into a single planning document that has two columns:

| What V3 Claims (Test Hypothesis) | What Code Must Do (Required Fix) |
|---|---|
| "V3 retrieves overriding clauses that TRAG misses" | Add Notwithstanding + exception patterns to cross-ref regex |
| "V3 preserves definition chunks that TRAG prunes" | Change bridge-node protection to type-based |
| "V3 resolves multi-hop queries in 1 pass vs TRAG needing N queries" | Build iterative TRAG agent + information unit checklists |
| "Evaluated using industry-standard RAGAS metrics" | Integrate `ragas` library, create ground truth |
| etc. | etc. |

This way you **never fix something without knowing which test it serves**, and you **never design a test without knowing if the code can support it**.

## So Is Your Approach Valid?

**80% valid.** The step-by-step discipline is correct. The separation of "fix" vs "test" is correct. The only adjustment is: **do a lightweight test design pass FIRST** (just the hypotheses and metric list — 1 hour of work), then do fixes, then flesh out the full test protocol with annotations. This avoids wasted effort and ensures every fix maps to a testable claim.

Want me to produce that combined plan — the mapping of claims → required fixes → tests → what's needed for each? That would be the single document you work from for the rest of the project.

```plaintext
1. Design tests & hypotheses (what are you claiming?)
         ↓
2. Create ground truth annotations (what does "correct" look like?)
         ↓
3. Fix the code (make the system work as claimed)
         ↓
4. Run tests & collect results
         ↓
5. Statistical analysis & write-up
```

### Questions I Need Answered

**1. Comparison scope: who is V3 competing against?**

Your `terminal_comparison.py` runs V3 vs Traditional RAG vs GraphRAG (3-way). But your testing protocol only discusses V3 vs Traditional RAG. 

- **Option A:** V3 vs Traditional RAG only (simpler, tighter narrative, easier to annotate)
- **Option B:** V3 vs Traditional RAG vs SimpleGraphRAG (3-way — shows V3 beats both)

Which do you want?

**2. Query preprocessing: LLM-based or rule-based?**

Right now the optimizer uses rule-based preprocessing (the `ParserStub` fallback). You *could* wire in the LLM for smarter subquery decomposition, but that adds an LLM call to V3's pipeline and weakens the "single LLM call" efficiency claim.

- **Option A:** Keep rule-based (claim: "V3 uses zero extra LLM calls for decomposition — pure graph logic")
- **Option B:** Wire in LLM preprocessing (claim: "V3 uses one cheap preprocessing call + one generation call, still fewer than iterative TRAG")

Which fits your intended narrative better?

**3. How many CUAD contracts are you willing to manually annotate?**

This determines the statistical power of your results. The annotation work is: read a contract, write 6-8 queries, extract the correct answers with section references, and build the information-unit checklists.

- **Minimum viable:** 3 contracts × 8 queries = 24 data points (tight, but workable with large effect sizes)
- **Solid:** 5 contracts × 8 queries = 40 data points (good statistical power)
- **Strong:** 8 contracts × 6 queries = 48 data points (publishable)

How much time do you realistically have for annotation?

**4. What LLM are you using for V3's final generation?**

Your config says `llm_provider: openai` with `openai_model: gpt-3.5-turbo`. Is this what you intend for the final evaluation, or are you planning to switch to GPT-4o-mini / Gemini for better answer quality? This matters because:
- If both V3 and TRAG use the same LLM, the comparison is fair (isolates retrieval quality)
- If they use different LLMs, you're conflating retrieval quality with generation quality

Just confirming: both systems will use the same LLM, correct?

**5. Budget constraint for evaluation API calls?**

Running RAGAS on 40 queries × 2 systems × 4 metrics ≈ 320 LLM judge calls. Roughly:
- GPT-4o-mini: ~$1-2
- GPT-4o: ~$5-8

Is this fine, or do you have a hard budget limit?

## 3. What "Manual Annotation" Means (This Is Critical)

**Manual annotation does NOT mean asking an LLM.** It means **you, the human researcher, read the actual contract text and extract the correct answers yourself.**

Here's why this is non-negotiable for research:

- If you use GPT to generate ground truth answers, and then use GPT (via RAGAS) to evaluate V3/TRAG against those answers, your entire evaluation is **LLM judging LLM against LLM's own output**. A reviewer will call this circular and reject the paper.
- Ground truth must be **independently verifiable**. A reader of your paper should be able to open the same CUAD contract, go to the section you cited, and confirm the answer is correct.

### What the process actually looks like:

**Step 1:** I give you query templates for each trap type (yes, I will provide these).

**Step 2:** You pick 8 CUAD contracts from your `full_contract_txt` folder.

**Step 3:** For each contract, you **open the .txt file and read it** (you don't need to read every word — skim for key sections: Definitions, Indemnification, Termination, Liability, IP Rights, Confidentiality).

**Step 4:** For each query template, you fill in the specifics based on what the contract actually says. For example:

| Field | You Write |
|-------|-----------|
| Query | "What is the maximum liability cap for IP infringement claims under this agreement?" |
| Ground Truth Answer | "Under Section 8.2, the Company's aggregate liability for IP infringement claims is capped at $2,000,000. However, per Section 12.3, this cap does not apply in cases of willful infringement or fraud." |
| Required Sections | ["Section 8.2", "Section 12.3"] |
| Information Units | ["liability cap exists", "cap amount is $2M", "cap is in Section 8.2", "exception for willful infringement", "exception is in Section 12.3"] |
| Trap Type | A (Invisible Exception) |

**Step 5:** The information units checklist is what makes the Tokens-to-Truth experiment work. Each unit is a binary yes/no: did the system's answer include this fact?

### Can an LLM help at all?

Yes, but only as a **search assistant**, not as the annotator:
- You CAN paste a contract into an LLM and ask "Which sections discuss indemnification?" to help you find relevant sections faster (like Ctrl+F but smarter)
- You CANNOT use the LLM's answer as your ground truth
- You MUST read the actual sections yourself and verify

**Per contract, this takes roughly 30–45 minutes** (skim the contract, write 6 queries with answers). For 8 contracts, that's about 4–6 hours of work total. This is the most important work in the entire project — everything else is code.

---

## 2. LLM-Based Preprocessing — Confirmed

Good, this is a clear design choice. V3's claim becomes:

> *"V3 uses an LLM preprocessing call to decompose complex queries into structured entities and subqueries, then navigates the pre-computed document graph via edge logic. This upfront investment in query understanding enables single-pass retrieval of structurally distant but legally critical clauses."*

The tradeoff (one extra LLM call) is justified for the use case. Latency is acceptable because correctness matters more for legal applications. This actually strengthens the paper's narrative — "we trade a small amount of compute for dramatically better retrieval quality."

---

## 4. Choosing the Right LLM Model

You need to choose for two purposes: **(a) the model integrated into V3/TRAG for answer generation**, and **(b) the model used by RAGAS for evaluation judging.**

### For V3/TRAG Generation (both systems use the same model)

| Model | Context Window | Legal Reasoning | Cost per 1M tokens (input) | Recommendation |
|-------|---------------|----------------|---------------------------|----------------|
| GPT-3.5-turbo (current) | 16K | Adequate | ~$0.50 | ❌ Weakest reasoning, fine for simple queries but will struggle with legal nuance |
| **GPT-4o-mini** | **128K** | **Good** | **~$0.15** | **✅ Best value — strong reasoning, cheap, large context window** |
| GPT-4o | 128K | Excellent | ~$2.50 | Overkill for this use case, expensive |
| Gemini 2.0 Flash | 1M | Good | ~$0.075 | ✅ Cheapest, good reasoning, massive context window |

**My recommendation: GPT-4o-mini** for these reasons:
- Your CSS token budget is 2000 tokens, so you don't need a massive context window — but 128K gives you headroom for the query preprocessor call and safety margin
- GPT-4o-mini has significantly better legal reasoning than GPT-3.5-turbo (it's based on GPT-4 architecture)
- It's actually **cheaper** than GPT-3.5-turbo per token
- It's the most commonly used model in recent RAG papers, so reviewers will find it reasonable

**Gemini 2.0 Flash** is a viable alternative if you want to minimize cost further. But using an OpenAI model for everything (generation + RAGAS evaluation) keeps the setup simpler.

### For RAGAS Evaluation Judging

Use **GPT-4o-mini** here too. The RAGAS library defaults to OpenAI models, and GPT-4o-mini is the standard choice for evaluation. Using the same model family (but in a different role — judge vs generator) is accepted practice.

### How to determine what's "ideal"?

For a research paper, the honest approach is: **pick one reasonable model and justify it.** You don't need to prove it's optimal. You just need to state:

> *"We used GPT-4o-mini for all generation tasks based on its strong performance on legal reasoning benchmarks and cost-efficiency. Both V3 and Traditional RAG use identical LLM configurations to isolate the effect of retrieval architecture."*

If a reviewer asks "what about other models?", that's future work, not a flaw.

# STEP 1: V3 Research Evaluation — Master Plan

## System Under Test

**V3 Graph-RAG** vs **Traditional RAG**, both using GPT-4o-mini, evaluated on CUAD legal contracts.

**V3's Core Thesis:** *V3 internalizes document structure (cross-references, defined terms, section relationships) into a pre-computed graph, enabling single-pass retrieval of structurally distant but legally critical clauses that vector-only systems miss.*

---

## PART A: Claims, Hypotheses & Required Fixes

Each row is: what you're claiming → what the code must do → what's broken → the fix.

---

### CLAIM 1: "V3 retrieves overriding/exception clauses that TRAG misses"
**(Trap A — Invisible Exception)**

**Hypothesis:** When a contract clause is overridden by a distant "Notwithstanding" provision, V3 retrieves both the main clause and the exception; TRAG retrieves only the main clause.

**What the code must do:**
1. `edge_builder.py` must detect "Notwithstanding Section X" as a cross-reference pattern so that an edge is created between the overriding clause and the overridden clause
2. `expand.py` must follow that edge during graph expansion to pull in the exception clause
3. `contradiction_flagger.py` must inject a warning into the final prompt so the LLM pays attention to the override

**What's currently broken:**

| Component | Status | Issue |
|-----------|--------|-------|
| Cross-ref regex in `edge_builder.py` | ⚠️ Partial | "Notwithstanding" is NOT in `CROSS_REF_PATTERNS` trigger words (line 26). Only catches `subject to\|pursuant to\|as defined in\|see\|under\|per`. |
| Edge traversal in `expand.py` | ✅ Working | Follows pre-computed cross-ref edges correctly |
| Contradiction flagger | ⚠️ Built but unwired | `contradiction_flagger.py` exists with correct patterns (line 20-29), but is **never imported or called** in the V3 pipeline. Only imported in `test_batch4.py`. |
| V3 prompt builder | ❌ Missing | `_build_v3_prompt()` in `terminal_comparison.py` (line 67-75) builds a plain prompt. It does NOT call `build_contradiction_annotation()` to inject warnings. |

**Required Fixes:**
- **Fix A1:** Add `notwithstanding|except as provided in|without limiting|provided however` to the first regex in `CROSS_REF_PATTERNS` in `edge_builder.py`
- **Fix A2:** Wire `build_contradiction_annotation()` into the V3 prompt builder (call it on the optimized graph, prepend the warning to the prompt)

---

### CLAIM 2: "V3 preserves structurally important definition chunks that TRAG misses"
**(Trap B — Distant Definition)**

**Hypothesis:** When a clause uses a defined term (e.g., "Change of Control Event") whose definition is 40 pages away, V3 retrieves both the clause and the definition; TRAG retrieves only the clause because the definition has low query similarity.

**What the code must do:**
1. `edge_builder.py` must detect defined-term links (`"X" means...`) and create edges
2. `expand.py` must follow those edges
3. `prune.py` must NOT prune definition nodes even if they have low query relevance (bridge-node protection)

**What's currently broken:**

| Component | Status | Issue |
|-----------|--------|-------|
| Defined-term detection in `edge_builder.py` | ✅ Working | `DEFINED_TERM_PATTERN` (line 38) and `_has_cross_reference` (line 194-207) correctly detect `"X" means...` links |
| Edge traversal in `expand.py` | ✅ Working | Follows all cross-ref and defined-term edges |
| Bridge-node protection in `prune.py` | ⚠️ Too strict | Requires `degree >= 3 AND has_cross_ref_edges` (line 68). A definition node typically links to only 1-2 clauses, so degree < 3. It gets pruned anyway. |

**Required Fixes:**
- **Fix B1:** Change bridge-node protection in `prune.py` to protect ANY node with `defined_term` or `cross_ref` edge types, regardless of degree

---

### CLAIM 3: "V3 retrieves diverse, complete information for multi-component queries"
**(Trap C — Scattered Components)**

**Hypothesis:** When a query asks for a cumulative list spread across multiple sections (e.g., "List all termination triggers"), V3 retrieves from all relevant sections. TRAG clusters around the single most similar section.

**What the code must do:**
1. `query_preprocessor.py` must decompose the query into subqueries using an LLM
2. `optimizer.py` must use those subqueries for entity-based multi-hop retrieval
3. `css/calculator.py`'s `subquery_coverage` must keep chunks that answer different subqueries (diversity enforcement)

**What's currently broken:**

| Component | Status | Issue |
|-----------|--------|-------|
| Query preprocessor | ⚠️ Falls back to rules | `preprocess_query()` is called in `optimizer.py` (line 64) and `calculator.py` (line 189) WITHOUT an `llm_client` argument, so it always uses `ParserStub` fallback which generates naive "What is X?" subqueries |
| Entity-based retrieval | ✅ Working | `_build_initial_retrieval_graph` (line 37-142 in optimizer.py) does multi-term FAISS search |
| Subquery coverage CSS | ✅ Working | `compute_subquery_coverage` (line 176-223 in calculator.py) correctly measures multi-subquery coverage |

**Required Fixes:**
- **Fix C1:** Create an LLM client wrapper that is compatible with `preprocess_query()`'s expected interface (a `.call(prompt, system, temperature)` method) using the existing OpenAI client
- **Fix C2:** Pass this LLM client into `preprocess_query()` calls in both `optimizer.py` and `calculator.py`

---

### CLAIM 4: "V3 achieves equivalent or better accuracy in fewer total tokens/iterations"
**(Tokens-to-Truth — Core Experiment)**

**Hypothesis:** V3 satisfies all information units of a complex query in a single retrieval+generation pass. TRAG requires multiple iterative queries to reach the same coverage, using more total tokens.

**What the code must do:**
1. An `IterativeTraditionalRAG` class must exist that can re-query when the answer is incomplete
2. A cost tracker must record tokens per iteration for both systems
3. An information-unit evaluator must check which units each answer covers

**What's currently broken:**

| Component | Status | Issue |
|-----------|--------|-------|
| Iterative TRAG | ❌ Missing | `TraditionalRAG` class is single-shot only. No iteration loop exists. |
| Cost tracker | ✅ Working | `CostTracker` and `ComparisonCostTracker` are complete |
| Information-unit evaluator | ❌ Missing | No mechanism to check answer against a checklist of information units |

**Required Fixes:**
- **Fix D1:** Build `IterativeTraditionalRAG` class — a loop that: runs TRAG → uses an LLM evaluator to check which information units are missing → generates a follow-up query → re-retrieves → repeats until all units are found or max iterations reached
- **Fix D2:** Build an information-unit evaluator function that takes an answer + a checklist and returns which items are covered (can be LLM-based or keyword-based)

---

### CLAIM 5: "Evaluated using industry-standard RAGAS metrics with ground truth"
**(Academic Credibility)**

**What the code must do:**
1. Use the real `ragas` library for Faithfulness, Answer Relevancy, Context Precision, Context Recall
2. Feed ground-truth annotations
3. Compute statistical significance across all queries

**What's currently broken:**

| Component | Status | Issue |
|-----------|--------|-------|
| RAGAS library | ❌ Not installed | Not in `requirements.txt`. Custom approximations are used instead. |
| Ground truth dataset | ❌ Missing | `cuad_eval_dataset.py` has keyword lists, not full reference answers |
| Statistical testing | ❌ Missing | No significance tests, confidence intervals, or effect sizes |

**Required Fixes:**
- **Fix E1:** Add `ragas` and `datasets` to `requirements.txt`, install them
- **Fix E2:** Create ground truth annotation file (your manual work — 8 contracts × 6 queries)
- **Fix E3:** Build an automated benchmark runner that runs all queries, collects scores, computes statistics (scipy)

## PART B: Tests to Run

### Test Suite 1: Trap A — Invisible Exception (8 queries)

**What it proves:** V3 retrieves overriding clauses; TRAG doesn't.

| Metric | How Measured | Expected Result |
|--------|-------------|-----------------|
| Exception Retrieval Rate | Does the retrieved context contain the overriding section? (binary, from ground truth checklist) | V3 ≈ 80-100%, TRAG ≈ 10-30% |
| RAGAS Context Recall | Does context cover all ground truth claims? | V3 >> TRAG |
| RAGAS Faithfulness | Is the answer grounded in context? | V3 ≥ TRAG (TRAG may hallucinate the exception) |
| Fatal Error Rate | Is the answer legally incomplete/incorrect because the override was missed? (LLM judge or manual check) | V3 ≈ 0-20%, TRAG ≈ 60-90% |

**Query template examples (you fill in specifics per contract):**
1. "What is the maximum liability for [X] under this agreement?"  *(where an exception clause overrides the cap)*
2. "What are the indemnification obligations regarding [Y]?"  *(where a Notwithstanding clause limits indemnification)*

**Ground truth required:** For each query, the main clause section + the overriding exception section + the information units.

---

### Test Suite 2: Trap B — Distant Definition (8 queries)

**What it proves:** V3 retrieves defined-term definitions; TRAG doesn't.

| Metric | How Measured | Expected Result |
|--------|-------------|-----------------|
| Definition Retrieval Rate | Does context contain the definition of the key defined term? (binary) | V3 ≈ 70-90%, TRAG ≈ 10-30% |
| RAGAS Context Recall | Full coverage check | V3 >> TRAG |
| RAGAS Answer Relevancy | Does the answer correctly use the defined term's meaning? | V3 >> TRAG |
| Hallucination Rate | Does the answer fabricate a definition? (LLM judge) | V3 ≈ low, TRAG ≈ high |

**Query template examples:**
1. "What restrictions apply to a [Defined Term] under the [restrictive covenant section]?"
2. "Under what circumstances does a [Defined Term] trigger [consequence]?"

**Ground truth required:** The clause using the term + the definition section + the actual definition text.

---

### Test Suite 3: Trap C — Scattered Components (8 queries)

**What it proves:** V3 retrieves from diverse sections; TRAG clusters around one.

| Metric | How Measured | Expected Result |
|--------|-------------|-----------------|
| Component Coverage | What fraction of information units are in the context? | V3 ≈ 80-100%, TRAG ≈ 40-60% |
| Section Diversity | How many distinct contract sections appear in context? | V3 > TRAG |
| RAGAS Context Precision | Is context useful, not redundant? | V3 > TRAG |
| Information Redundancy | Cosine similarity between retrieved chunks (lower = more diverse) | V3 < TRAG |

**Query template examples:**
1. "List all the events that allow termination for cause under this agreement."
2. "What are all the conditions precedent to closing?"
3. "Summarize all the survival provisions."

**Ground truth required:** The full list of sections that contain relevant components + the information units from each.

---

### Test Suite 4: Tokens-to-Truth Iteration Test (all 24 Trap queries reused)

**What it proves:** V3 is more token-efficient for complex queries.

| Metric | How Measured | Expected Result |
|--------|-------------|-----------------|
| Iterations to Full Coverage | How many TRAG iterations to match V3's info-unit coverage? | V3 = 1, TRAG = 2-5 |
| Total Prompt Tokens | Cumulative tokens sent to LLM | V3 < TRAG (iterative) |
| Total Completion Tokens | Cumulative tokens generated | V3 < TRAG |
| End-to-End Latency | Wall-clock time to final answer | V3 < TRAG (iterative) |

**No extra annotation needed** — reuses the same ground truth from Suites 1-3.

---

### Test Suite 5: RAGAS Aggregate (all 24 queries)

**What it proves:** V3 is better across standard academic metrics.

| RAGAS Metric | Expected Result |
|--------------|-----------------|
| Faithfulness | V3 ≥ TRAG |
| Answer Relevancy | V3 ≈ TRAG (both use same LLM) |
| Context Precision | V3 > TRAG (CSS optimizer prunes noise) |
| Context Recall | V3 >> TRAG (graph traversal retrieves more relevant context) |

**Statistical reporting:** Paired Wilcoxon signed-rank test per metric, Cohen's d effect size, 95% confidence intervals, Bonferroni correction for multiple comparisons.
